<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Erosion</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Natural processes that remove soil and rock</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Erosion_(disambiguation)" title="Erosion (disambiguation)">Erosion (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eroding_rill_in_field_in_eastern_Germany.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1864" data-file-width="1275" decoding="async" height="322" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Eroding_rill_in_field_in_eastern_Germany.jpg/220px-Eroding_rill_in_field_in_eastern_Germany.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Eroding_rill_in_field_in_eastern_Germany.jpg/330px-Eroding_rill_in_field_in_eastern_Germany.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Eroding_rill_in_field_in_eastern_Germany.jpg/440px-Eroding_rill_in_field_in_eastern_Germany.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eroding_rill_in_field_in_eastern_Germany.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An actively eroding <a href="/wiki/Rill" title="Rill">rill</a> on an <a href="/wiki/Intensive_farming" title="Intensive farming">intensively-farmed</a> field in <a href="/wiki/New_states_of_Germany" title="New states of Germany">eastern</a> <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a></div></div></div>
<p><b>Erosion</b> is the action of surface processes (such as <a href="/wiki/Surface_runoff" title="Surface runoff">water flow</a> or <a href="/wiki/Wind" title="Wind">wind</a>) that removes <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">rock</a>, or dissolved material from one location on the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_crust#Crust" title="Earth's crust">Earth's crust</a>, and then <a href="/wiki/Sediment_transport" title="Sediment transport">transports</a> it to another location where it is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deposit_(geology)" title="Deposit (geology)">deposited</a>. Erosion is distinct from <a href="/wiki/Weathering" title="Weathering">weathering</a> which involves no movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brittanica_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brittanica-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Removal of rock or soil as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clastic" title="Clastic">clastic</a> <a href="/wiki/Sediment" title="Sediment">sediment</a> is referred to as <i>physical</i> or <i>mechanical</i> erosion; this contrasts with <i>chemical</i> erosion, where soil or rock material is removed from an area by <a href="/wiki/Solvation" title="Solvation">dissolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Eroded <a href="/wiki/Sediment" title="Sediment">sediment</a> or solutes may be transported just a few millimetres, or for thousands of kilometres.
</p><p>Agents of erosion include <a href="/wiki/Rain" title="Rain">rainfall</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hysteretic_sediment_fluxes_in_ra_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hysteretic_sediment_fluxes_in_ra-4">[4]</a></sup> bedrock wear in <a href="/wiki/River" title="River">rivers</a>; <a href="/wiki/Coastal_erosion" title="Coastal erosion">coastal erosion</a> by the sea and <a href="/wiki/Wind_wave" title="Wind wave">waves</a>; <a href="/wiki/Glacier" title="Glacier">glacial</a> plucking, <a href="/wiki/Abrasion_(geology)" title="Abrasion (geology)">abrasion</a>, and scour; areal flooding; <a href="/wiki/Aeolian_processes" title="Aeolian processes">wind</a> abrasion; <a href="/wiki/Groundwater" title="Groundwater">groundwater</a> processes; and <a href="/wiki/Mass_wasting" title="Mass wasting">mass movement</a> processes in steep <a href="/wiki/Landscape" title="Landscape">landscapes</a> like <a href="/wiki/Landslide" title="Landslide">landslides</a> and <a href="/wiki/Debris_flow" title="Debris flow">debris flows</a>. The rates at which such processes act control how fast a surface is eroded. Typically, physical erosion proceeds fastest on steeply sloping surfaces, and rates may also be sensitive to some climatically-controlled properties including amounts of water supplied (e.g., by rain), storminess, wind speed, wave <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fetch_(geography)" title="Fetch (geography)">fetch</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_temperature" title="Atmospheric temperature">atmospheric temperature (</a>especially for some ice-related processes). <a href="/wiki/Feedback" title="Feedback">Feedbacks</a> are also possible between rates of erosion and the amount of eroded material that is already carried by, for example, a river or glacier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hallet1981_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hallet1981-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SklarDietrich2004_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-SklarDietrich2004-6">[6]</a></sup> The transport of eroded materials from their original location is followed by <a href="/wiki/Deposition_(geology)" title="Deposition (geology)">deposition</a>, which is arrival and <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Emplacement" title="Emplacement">emplacement</a> of material at a new location.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brittanica_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brittanica-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>While erosion is a natural process, human activities have increased by 10-40 times the rate at which erosion is occurring globally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>  At agriculture sites in the <a href="/wiki/Appalachian_Mountains" title="Appalachian Mountains">Appalachian Mountains</a>, intensive farming practices have caused erosion at up to 100 times the natural rate of erosion in the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>  Excessive (or accelerated) erosion causes both "on-site" and "off-site" problems. On-site impacts include decreases in <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_productivity" title="Agricultural productivity">agricultural productivity</a> and (on <a href="/wiki/Natural_landscape" title="Natural landscape">natural landscapes</a>) <a href="/wiki/Ecological_collapse" title="Ecological collapse">ecological collapse</a>, both because of loss of the nutrient-rich upper <a href="/wiki/Soil_horizon" title="Soil horizon">soil layers</a>. In some cases, this leads to <a href="/wiki/Desertification" title="Desertification">desertification</a>. Off-site effects include <a href="/wiki/Sediment#Erosion_and_agricultural_sediment_delivery_to_rivers" title="Sediment">sedimentation of waterways</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eutrophication" title="Eutrophication">eutrophication</a> of <a href="/wiki/Body_of_water" title="Body of water">water bodies</a>, as well as sediment-related damage to roads and houses. Water and wind erosion are the two primary causes of <a href="/wiki/Land_degradation" title="Land degradation">land degradation</a>; combined, they are responsible for about 84% of the global extent of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Degraded_land" title="Degraded land">degraded land</a>, making excessive erosion one of the most significant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_problems" title="Environmental problems">environmental problems</a> worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BlancoConservation_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-BlancoConservation-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 2">: 2 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toy-2002-p1_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-toy-2002-p1-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 1">: 1 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Apollo_10_(4)_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Apollo_10_(4)-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Intensive_farming" title="Intensive farming">Intensive agriculture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Road" title="Road">roads</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anthropogenic_climate_change" title="Anthropogenic climate change">anthropogenic climate change</a> and <a href="/wiki/Urban_sprawl" title="Urban sprawl">urban sprawl</a> are amongst the most significant human activities in regard to their effect on stimulating erosion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> However, there are many <a href="#Prevention_and_remediation">prevention and remediation</a> practices that can curtail or limit erosion of vulnerable soils.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KharazaArch.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1456" data-file-width="1908" decoding="async" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/KharazaArch.jpg/220px-KharazaArch.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/KharazaArch.jpg/330px-KharazaArch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/KharazaArch.jpg/440px-KharazaArch.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:KharazaArch.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Natural_arch" title="Natural arch">natural arch</a> produced by the wind erosion of differentially weathered rock in Jebel Kharaz, <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E5%A4%A7%E8%BF%9E%E5%9B%BD%E5%AE%B6%E5%9C%B0%E8%B4%A8%E5%85%AC%E5%9B%AD9-%E6%B5%B7%E8%9A%80%E5%B4%96.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="4608" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/%E5%A4%A7%E8%BF%9E%E5%9B%BD%E5%AE%B6%E5%9C%B0%E8%B4%A8%E5%85%AC%E5%9B%AD9-%E6%B5%B7%E8%9A%80%E5%B4%96.JPG/220px-%E5%A4%A7%E8%BF%9E%E5%9B%BD%E5%AE%B6%E5%9C%B0%E8%B4%A8%E5%85%AC%E5%9B%AD9-%E6%B5%B7%E8%9A%80%E5%B4%96.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/%E5%A4%A7%E8%BF%9E%E5%9B%BD%E5%AE%B6%E5%9C%B0%E8%B4%A8%E5%85%AC%E5%9B%AD9-%E6%B5%B7%E8%9A%80%E5%B4%96.JPG/330px-%E5%A4%A7%E8%BF%9E%E5%9B%BD%E5%AE%B6%E5%9C%B0%E8%B4%A8%E5%85%AC%E5%9B%AD9-%E6%B5%B7%E8%9A%80%E5%B4%96.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/%E5%A4%A7%E8%BF%9E%E5%9B%BD%E5%AE%B6%E5%9C%B0%E8%B4%A8%E5%85%AC%E5%9B%AD9-%E6%B5%B7%E8%9A%80%E5%B4%96.JPG/440px-%E5%A4%A7%E8%BF%9E%E5%9B%BD%E5%AE%B6%E5%9C%B0%E8%B4%A8%E5%85%AC%E5%9B%AD9-%E6%B5%B7%E8%9A%80%E5%B4%96.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E5%A4%A7%E8%BF%9E%E5%9B%BD%E5%AE%B6%E5%9C%B0%E8%B4%A8%E5%85%AC%E5%9B%AD9-%E6%B5%B7%E8%9A%80%E5%B4%96.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A wave-like sea cliff produced by <a href="/wiki/Coastal_erosion" title="Coastal erosion">coastal erosion</a>, in Jinshitan Coastal National Geopark, <a href="/wiki/Dalian" title="Dalian">Dalian</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liaoning_Province" title="Liaoning Province">Liaoning Province</a>, China</div></div></div>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"><a href="#Erosion_at_various_scales"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Erosion at various scales</span></a>
<ul>
<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#Mountain_ranges"><span class="tocnumber">3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Mountain ranges</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#Soils"><span class="tocnumber">3.2</span> <span class="toctext">Soils</span></a></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#Consequences_of_human-made_soil_erosion"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Consequences of human-made soil erosion</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_processes">Physical processes</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rainfall_and_surface_runoff">Rainfall and surface runoff</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Water_and_soil_splashed_by_the_impact_of_a_single_raindrop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="571" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Water_and_soil_splashed_by_the_impact_of_a_single_raindrop.jpg/220px-Water_and_soil_splashed_by_the_impact_of_a_single_raindrop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Water_and_soil_splashed_by_the_impact_of_a_single_raindrop.jpg/330px-Water_and_soil_splashed_by_the_impact_of_a_single_raindrop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Water_and_soil_splashed_by_the_impact_of_a_single_raindrop.jpg/440px-Water_and_soil_splashed_by_the_impact_of_a_single_raindrop.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Water_and_soil_splashed_by_the_impact_of_a_single_raindrop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">Soil</a> and water being <a href="/wiki/Splash_(fluid_mechanics)" title="Splash (fluid mechanics)">splashed</a> by the impact of a single <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Raindrop" title="Raindrop">raindrop</a></div></div></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rainfall" title="Rainfall">Rainfall</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Surface_runoff" title="Surface runoff">surface runoff</a> which may result from rainfall, produces four main types of <a href="/wiki/Soil_erosion" title="Soil erosion">soil erosion</a>: <i>splash erosion</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Sheet_erosion" title="Sheet erosion">sheet erosion</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Rill" title="Rill">rill erosion</a></i>, and <i>gully erosion</i>. Splash erosion is generally seen as the first and least severe stage in the soil erosion process, which is followed by sheet erosion, then rill erosion and finally gully erosion (the most severe of the four).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toy-2002-p1_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-toy-2002-p1-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 60–61">: 60–61 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <i>splash erosion</i>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rainfall#Raindrop_impacts" title="Rainfall">impact of a falling raindrop</a> creates a small crater in the <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soil</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fig._4_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fig._4-14">[14]</a></sup> ejecting soil particles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hysteretic_sediment_fluxes_in_ra_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hysteretic_sediment_fluxes_in_ra-4">[4]</a></sup> The distance these soil particles travel can be as much as 0.6 m (two feet) vertically and 1.5 m (five feet) horizontally on level ground.
</p><p>If <a href="/wiki/Surface_runoff#Saturation_excess_overland_flow" title="Surface runoff">the soil is saturated</a>, or if the rainfall rate is <a href="/wiki/Surface_runoff#Infiltration_excess_overland_flow" title="Surface runoff">greater than the rate at which water can infiltrate</a> into the soil, surface runoff occurs. If the runoff has sufficient <a href="/wiki/Fluid_dynamics" title="Fluid dynamics">flow energy</a>, it will <a href="/wiki/Sediment_transport" title="Sediment transport">transport</a> loosened soil particles (<a href="/wiki/Sediment" title="Sediment">sediment</a>) down the slope.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAO-1965-pp23-25_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAO-1965-pp23-25-15">[15]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Sheet_erosion" title="Sheet erosion">Sheet erosion</a></i> is the transport of loosened soil particles by overland flow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAO-1965-pp23-25_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-FAO-1965-pp23-25-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rummu_aherainem%C3%A4gi2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2924" data-file-width="8374" decoding="async" height="77" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Rummu_aherainem%C3%A4gi2.jpg/220px-Rummu_aherainem%C3%A4gi2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Rummu_aherainem%C3%A4gi2.jpg/330px-Rummu_aherainem%C3%A4gi2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Rummu_aherainem%C3%A4gi2.jpg/440px-Rummu_aherainem%C3%A4gi2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rummu_aherainem%C3%A4gi2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Spoil_tip" title="Spoil tip">spoil tip</a> covered in rills and gullies due to erosion processes caused by rainfall: <a href="/wiki/Rummu" title="Rummu">Rummu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a></div></div></div>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Rill" title="Rill">Rill</a> erosion</i> refers to the development of small, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ephemeral" title="Ephemeral">ephemeral</a> concentrated flow paths which function as both sediment source and <a href="/wiki/Sediment" title="Sediment">sediment</a> delivery systems for erosion on hillslopes. Generally, where water erosion rates on disturbed upland areas are greatest, rills are active. Flow depths in rills are typically of the order of a few centimetres (about an inch) or less and along-channel slopes may be quite steep. This means that rills exhibit <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydraulic" title="Hydraulic">hydraulic</a> physics very different from water flowing through the deeper, wider channels of streams and rivers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="gully_erosion"></span><span class="anchor" id="ephemeral_gully_erosion"></span>
<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gully_erosion" title="Gully erosion">Gully erosion</a></i> occurs when runoff water accumulates and rapidly flows in narrow channels during or immediately after heavy rains or melting snow, removing soil to a considerable depth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boardman_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boardman-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>  A gully is distinguished from a rill based on a critical cross-sectional area of at least one square foot, i.e. the size of a channel that can no longer be erased via normal tillage operations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>Extreme gully erosion can progress to formation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Badland" title="Badland">badlands</a>. These form under conditions of high relief on <a href="/wiki/Erodability" title="Erodability">easily eroded bedrock</a> in climates favorable to erosion. Conditions or disturbances that limit the growth of protective vegetation (<a href="/wiki/Biorhexistasy" title="Biorhexistasy">rhexistasy</a>) are a key element of badland formation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rivers_and_streams">Rivers and streams</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information on water's erosive ability: <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_action" title="Hydraulic action">Hydraulic action</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dobbingstone_Burn_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1291882.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="426" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Dobbingstone_Burn_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1291882.jpg/220px-Dobbingstone_Burn_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1291882.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Dobbingstone_Burn_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1291882.jpg/330px-Dobbingstone_Burn_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1291882.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Dobbingstone_Burn_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1291882.jpg/440px-Dobbingstone_Burn_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1291882.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dobbingstone_Burn_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1291882.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dobbingstone <a href="/wiki/Burn_(landform)" title="Burn (landform)">Burn</a>, Scotland, showing two different types of erosion affecting the same place. Valley erosion is occurring due to the flow of the stream, and the boulders and stones (and much of the soil) that are lying on the stream's banks are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glacial_till" title="Glacial till">glacial till</a> that was left behind as ice age glaciers flowed over the terrain.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tauglbach_4.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3864" data-file-width="5152" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Tauglbach_4.JPG/220px-Tauglbach_4.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Tauglbach_4.JPG/330px-Tauglbach_4.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Tauglbach_4.JPG/440px-Tauglbach_4.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tauglbach_4.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Layers of <a href="/wiki/Chalk" title="Chalk">chalk</a> exposed by a river eroding through them</div></div></div>
<p><i>Valley</i> or <i>stream erosion</i> occurs with continued water flow along a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linear_feature_extraction" title="Linear feature extraction">linear feature.</a> The erosion is both <a href="/wiki/Downcutting" title="Downcutting">downward</a>, deepening the <a href="/wiki/Valley" title="Valley">valley</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Headward_erosion" title="Headward erosion">headward</a>, extending the valley into the hillside, creating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Head_Cut_(stream_geomorphology)" title="Head Cut (stream geomorphology)">head cuts</a> and steep banks. In the earliest stage of stream erosion, the erosive activity is dominantly vertical, the valleys have a typical V-shaped cross-section and the stream gradient is relatively steep. When some <a href="/wiki/Base_level" title="Base level">base level</a> is reached, the erosive activity switches to lateral erosion, which widens the valley floor and creates a narrow floodplain. The stream gradient becomes nearly flat, and lateral deposition of sediments becomes important as the stream <a href="/wiki/Meander" title="Meander">meanders</a> across the valley floor. In all stages of stream erosion, by far the most erosion occurs during times of flood when more and faster-moving water is available to carry a larger sediment load. In such processes, it is not the water alone that erodes: suspended abrasive particles, <a href="/wiki/Pebble" title="Pebble">pebbles</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Boulder" title="Boulder">boulders</a> can also act erosively as they traverse a surface, in a process known as <i>traction</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Bank_erosion" title="Bank erosion">Bank erosion</a></i> is the wearing away of the banks of a stream or river. This is distinguished from changes on the bed of the watercourse, which is referred to as <i>scour</i>. Erosion and <a href="/wiki/River_bank_failure" title="River bank failure">changes in the form of river banks</a> may be measured by inserting metal rods into the bank and marking the position of the bank surface along the rods at different times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Thermal erosion</i> is the result of melting and weakening <a href="/wiki/Permafrost" title="Permafrost">permafrost</a> due to moving water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nsidc_thermal_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-nsidc_thermal-24">[24]</a></sup> It can occur both along rivers and at the coast. Rapid <a href="/wiki/River_channel_migration" title="River channel migration">river channel migration</a> observed in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lena_River" title="Lena River">Lena River</a> of Siberia is due to thermal erosion, as these portions of the banks are composed of permafrost-cemented non-cohesive materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lena_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-lena-25">[25]</a></sup> Much of this erosion occurs as the weakened banks fail in large slumps. Thermal erosion also affects the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Ocean" title="Arctic Ocean">Arctic coast</a>, where wave action and near-shore temperatures combine to undercut permafrost bluffs along the shoreline and cause them to fail. Annual erosion rates along a 100-kilometre (62-mile) segment of the <a href="/wiki/Beaufort_Sea" title="Beaufort Sea">Beaufort Sea</a> shoreline averaged 5.6 metres (18 feet) per year from 1955 to 2002.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jones_arctic_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-jones_arctic-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most river erosion happens nearer to the mouth of a river. On a river bend, the longest least sharp side has slower moving water. Here deposits build up. On the narrowest sharpest side of the bend, there is faster moving water so this side tends to erode away mostly.
</p><p>Rapid erosion by a large river can remove enough sediments to produce a <a href="/wiki/River_anticline" title="River anticline">river anticline</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mont_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-mont-27">[27]</a></sup> as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isostatic_rebound" title="Isostatic rebound">isostatic rebound</a> raises rock beds unburdened by erosion of overlying beds.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coastal_erosion">Coastal erosion</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coastal_erosion" title="Coastal erosion">Coastal erosion</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Beach_evolution" title="Beach evolution">Beach evolution</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wavecut_platform_southerndown_pano.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="2393" decoding="async" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Wavecut_platform_southerndown_pano.jpg/220px-Wavecut_platform_southerndown_pano.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Wavecut_platform_southerndown_pano.jpg/330px-Wavecut_platform_southerndown_pano.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Wavecut_platform_southerndown_pano.jpg/440px-Wavecut_platform_southerndown_pano.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wavecut_platform_southerndown_pano.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wave_cut_platform" title="Wave cut platform">Wave cut platform</a> caused by erosion of cliffs by the sea, at <a href="/wiki/Southerndown" title="Southerndown">Southerndown</a> in South Wales</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Erosion_of_Boulder_Clay_in_Filey_Bay.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Erosion_of_Boulder_Clay_in_Filey_Bay.JPG/220px-Erosion_of_Boulder_Clay_in_Filey_Bay.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Erosion_of_Boulder_Clay_in_Filey_Bay.JPG/330px-Erosion_of_Boulder_Clay_in_Filey_Bay.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Erosion_of_Boulder_Clay_in_Filey_Bay.JPG/440px-Erosion_of_Boulder_Clay_in_Filey_Bay.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Erosion_of_Boulder_Clay_in_Filey_Bay.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Erosion of the <a href="/wiki/Boulder_clay" title="Boulder clay">boulder clay</a> (of <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> age) along cliffs of <a href="/wiki/Filey" title="Filey">Filey</a> Bay, Yorkshire, England</div></div></div>
<p>Shoreline erosion, which occurs on both exposed and sheltered coasts, primarily occurs through the action of currents and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ocean_surface_wave" title="Ocean surface wave">waves</a> but sea level (tidal) change can also play a role.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><video class="thumbimage" controls="" data-durationhint="61" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Sea_dune_Erosion_at_Talace,_Wales.webm" height="124" id="mwe_player_0" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Sea_dune_Erosion_at_Talace%2C_Wales.webm/220px--Sea_dune_Erosion_at_Talace%2C_Wales.webm.jpg" preload="none" width="220"><source data-bandwidth="134208" data-framerate="24" data-height="360" data-shorttitle="VP9 360P" data-title="VP9 (360P)" data-transcodekey="360p.vp9.webm" data-width="640" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/2/24/Sea_dune_Erosion_at_Talace%2C_Wales.webm/Sea_dune_Erosion_at_Talace%2C_Wales.webm.360p.vp9.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp9, opus"'/><source data-bandwidth="223648" data-framerate="24" data-height="360" data-shorttitle="WebM 360P" data-title="WebM (360P)" data-transcodekey="360p.webm" data-width="640" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/2/24/Sea_dune_Erosion_at_Talace%2C_Wales.webm/Sea_dune_Erosion_at_Talace%2C_Wales.webm.360p.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp8, vorbis"'/><source data-bandwidth="86672" data-framerate="24" data-height="120" data-shorttitle="VP9 120P" data-title="Lowest bandwidth VP9 (120P)" data-transcodekey="120p.vp9.webm" data-width="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/2/24/Sea_dune_Erosion_at_Talace%2C_Wales.webm/Sea_dune_Erosion_at_Talace%2C_Wales.webm.120p.vp9.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp9, opus"'/><source data-bandwidth="112736" data-framerate="24" data-height="160" data-shorttitle="WebM 160P" data-title="Low bandwidth WebM (160P)" data-transcodekey="160p.webm" data-width="284" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/2/24/Sea_dune_Erosion_at_Talace%2C_Wales.webm/Sea_dune_Erosion_at_Talace%2C_Wales.webm.160p.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp8, vorbis"'/><source data-bandwidth="95304" data-framerate="24" data-height="180" data-shorttitle="VP9 180P" data-title="Low bandwidth VP9 (180P)" data-transcodekey="180p.vp9.webm" data-width="320" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/2/24/Sea_dune_Erosion_at_Talace%2C_Wales.webm/Sea_dune_Erosion_at_Talace%2C_Wales.webm.180p.vp9.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp9, opus"'/><source data-bandwidth="104464" data-framerate="24" data-height="240" data-shorttitle="VP9 240P" data-title="Small VP9 (240P)" data-transcodekey="240p.vp9.webm" data-width="426" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/2/24/Sea_dune_Erosion_at_Talace%2C_Wales.webm/Sea_dune_Erosion_at_Talace%2C_Wales.webm.240p.vp9.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp9, opus"'/><source data-bandwidth="146496" data-framerate="24" data-height="240" data-shorttitle="WebM 240P" data-title="Small WebM (240P)" data-transcodekey="240p.webm" data-width="426" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/2/24/Sea_dune_Erosion_at_Talace%2C_Wales.webm/Sea_dune_Erosion_at_Talace%2C_Wales.webm.240p.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp8, vorbis"'/></video> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sea_dune_Erosion_at_Talace,_Wales.webm" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sea-dune erosion at <a href="/wiki/Talacre" title="Talacre">Talacre</a> beach, <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a></div></div></div>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_action" title="Hydraulic action">Hydraulic action</a></i> takes place when the air in a joint is suddenly compressed by a wave closing the entrance of the joint. This then cracks it. <i><a href="/wiki/Wave_pounding" title="Wave pounding">Wave pounding</a></i> is when the sheer energy of the wave hitting the cliff or rock breaks pieces off. <i><a href="/wiki/Abrasion_(geology)" title="Abrasion (geology)">Abrasion</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Corrasion" title="Corrasion">corrasion</a></i> is caused by waves launching sea load at the cliff. It is the most effective and rapid form of shoreline erosion (not to be confused with <i>corrosion</i>). <i><a href="/wiki/Corrosion" title="Corrosion">Corrosion</a></i> is the dissolving of rock by <a href="/wiki/Carbonic_acid" title="Carbonic acid">carbonic acid</a> in sea water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">Limestone</a> cliffs are particularly vulnerable to this kind of erosion. <i>Attrition</i> is where particles/sea load carried by the waves are worn down as they hit each other and the cliffs. This then makes the material easier to wash away. The material ends up as <a href="/wiki/Shingle_beach" title="Shingle beach">shingle</a> and sand. Another significant source of erosion, particularly on carbonate coastlines, is boring, scraping and grinding of organisms, a process termed <i><a href="/wiki/Bioerosion" title="Bioerosion">bioerosion</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sediment" title="Sediment">Sediment</a> is transported along the coast in the direction of the prevailing current (<a href="/wiki/Longshore_drift" title="Longshore drift">longshore drift</a>). When the upcurrent <a href="/wiki/Coastal_sediment_supply" title="Coastal sediment supply">supply of sediment</a> is less than the amount being carried away, erosion occurs. When the upcurrent amount of sediment is greater, sand or gravel banks will tend to form as a result of <a href="/wiki/Deposition_(geology)" title="Deposition (geology)">deposition</a>. These banks may slowly migrate along the coast in the direction of the longshore drift, alternately protecting and exposing parts of the coastline. Where there is a bend in the coastline, quite often a buildup of eroded material occurs forming a long narrow bank (a <a href="/wiki/Spit_(landform)" title="Spit (landform)">spit</a>). <a href="/wiki/Armor_(hydrology)" title="Armor (hydrology)">Armoured</a> beaches and submerged offshore <a href="/wiki/Shoal" title="Shoal">sandbanks</a> may also protect parts of a coastline from erosion. Over the years, as the shoals gradually shift, the erosion may be redirected to attack different parts of the shore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>Erosion of a coastal surface, followed by a fall in sea level, can produce a distinctive landform called a <a href="/wiki/Raised_beach" title="Raised beach">raised beach</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pinter2010_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pinter2010-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_erosion">Chemical erosion</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Karst_topography" title="Karst topography">Karst topography</a></div>
<p>Chemical erosion is the loss of matter in a landscape in the form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solutes" title="Solutes">solutes</a>. Chemical erosion is usually calculated from the solutes found in streams. <a href="/wiki/Anders_Rapp" title="Anders Rapp">Anders Rapp</a> pioneered the study of chemical erosion in his work about <a href="/wiki/K%C3%A4rkevagge" title="Kärkevagge">Kärkevagge</a> published in 1960.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>Formation of <a href="/wiki/Sinkhole" title="Sinkhole">sinkholes</a> and other features of karst topography is an example of extreme chemical erosion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Glaciers">Glaciers</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pirunpes%C3%A4_Jalasj%C3%A4rvi_8.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2376" data-file-width="3584" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Pirunpes%C3%A4_Jalasj%C3%A4rvi_8.JPG/220px-Pirunpes%C3%A4_Jalasj%C3%A4rvi_8.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Pirunpes%C3%A4_Jalasj%C3%A4rvi_8.JPG/330px-Pirunpes%C3%A4_Jalasj%C3%A4rvi_8.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Pirunpes%C3%A4_Jalasj%C3%A4rvi_8.JPG/440px-Pirunpes%C3%A4_Jalasj%C3%A4rvi_8.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pirunpes%C3%A4_Jalasj%C3%A4rvi_8.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/The_Devil%27s_Nest" title="The Devil's Nest">The Devil's Nest</a> (<i>Pirunpesä</i>), the deepest ground erosion in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> located in <a href="/wiki/Jalasj%C3%A4rvi" title="Jalasjärvi">Jalasjärvi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kurikka" title="Kurikka">Kurikka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MorainesLakeLouise.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2874" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/MorainesLakeLouise.JPG/220px-MorainesLakeLouise.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/MorainesLakeLouise.JPG/330px-MorainesLakeLouise.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/MorainesLakeLouise.JPG/440px-MorainesLakeLouise.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MorainesLakeLouise.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Glacial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moraines" title="Moraines">moraines</a> above <a href="/wiki/Lake_Louise,_Alberta" title="Lake Louise, Alberta">Lake Louise</a>, in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alberta,_Canada" title="Alberta, Canada">Alberta, Canada</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Glacier" title="Glacier">Glaciers</a> erode predominantly by three different processes: abrasion/scouring, <a href="/wiki/Plucking_(glaciation)" title="Plucking (glaciation)">plucking</a>, and ice thrusting. In an abrasion process, debris in the basal ice scrapes along the bed, polishing and gouging the underlying rocks, similar to sandpaper on wood. Scientists have shown that, in addition to the role of temperature played in valley-deepening, other glaciological processes, such as erosion also control cross-valley variations. In a homogeneous bedrock erosion pattern, curved channel cross-section beneath the ice is created. Though the glacier continues to incise vertically, the shape of the channel beneath the ice eventually remain constant, reaching a U-shaped parabolic steady-state shape as we now see in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glaciated_valley" title="Glaciated valley">glaciated valleys</a>. Scientists also provide a numerical estimate of the time required for the ultimate formation of a steady-shaped <a href="/wiki/U-shaped_valley" title="U-shaped valley">U-shaped valley</a>—approximately 100,000 years. In a weak bedrock (containing material more erodible than the surrounding rocks) erosion pattern, on the contrary, the amount of over deepening is limited because ice velocities and erosion rates are reduced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>Glaciers can also cause pieces of bedrock to crack off in the process of plucking. In ice thrusting, the glacier freezes to its bed, then as it surges forward, it moves large sheets of frozen sediment at the base along with the glacier. This method produced some of the many thousands of lake basins that dot the edge of the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Shield" title="Canadian Shield">Canadian Shield</a>. Differences in the height of mountain ranges are not only being the result tectonic forces, such as rock uplift, but also local climate variations. Scientists use global analysis of topography to show that glacial erosion controls the maximum height of mountains, as the relief between mountain peaks and the snow line are generally confined to altitudes less than 1500 m.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>  The erosion caused by glaciers worldwide erodes mountains so effectively that the term <i><a href="/wiki/Glacial_buzzsaw" title="Glacial buzzsaw">glacial buzzsaw</a></i> has become widely used, which describes the limiting effect of glaciers on the height of mountain ranges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reference_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-reference-37">[37]</a></sup> As mountains grow higher, they generally allow for more glacial activity (especially in the <a href="/wiki/Accumulation_zone" title="Accumulation zone">accumulation zone</a> above the glacial equilibrium line altitude),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> which causes increased rates of erosion of the mountain, decreasing mass faster than <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isostatic_rebound" title="Isostatic rebound">isostatic rebound</a> can add to the mountain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> This provides a good example of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Negative_feedback_loop" title="Negative feedback loop">negative feedback loop</a>. Ongoing research is showing that while glaciers tend to decrease mountain size, in some areas, glaciers can actually reduce the rate of erosion, acting as a <i>glacial armor</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reference_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-reference-37">[37]</a></sup> Ice can not only erode mountains but also protect them from erosion. Depending on glacier regime, even steep alpine lands can be preserved through time with the help of ice. Scientists have proved this theory by sampling eight summits of northwestern Svalbard using Be10 and Al26, showing that northwestern Svalbard transformed from a glacier-erosion state under relatively mild glacial maxima temperature, to a glacier-armor state occupied by cold-based, protective ice during much colder glacial maxima temperatures as the Quaternary ice age progressed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>These processes, combined with erosion and transport by the water network beneath the glacier, leave behind <a href="/wiki/Glacial_landform" title="Glacial landform">glacial landforms</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Moraine" title="Moraine">moraines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Drumlin" title="Drumlin">drumlins</a>, ground moraine (till), kames, kame deltas, moulins, and <a href="/wiki/Glacial_erratic" title="Glacial erratic">glacial erratics</a> in their wake, typically at the terminus or during <a href="/wiki/Retreat_of_glaciers_since_1850" title="Retreat of glaciers since 1850">glacier retreat</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>The best-developed glacial valley morphology appears to be restricted to landscapes with low rock uplift rates (less than or equal to 2mm per year) and high relief, leading to long-turnover times. Where rock uplift rates exceed 2mm per year, glacial valley morphology has generally been significantly modified in postglacial time. Interplay of glacial erosion and tectonic forcing governs the morphologic impact of glaciations on active orogens, by both influencing their height, and by altering the patterns of erosion during subsequent glacial periods via a link between rock uplift and valley cross-sectional shape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Floods">Floods</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Seaton_Beach_after_heavy_rainfall.jpg"><img alt="The mouth of the River Seaton in Cornwall after heavy rainfall caused flooding in the area and cause a significant amount of the beach to erode" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1800" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="99" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Seaton_Beach_after_heavy_rainfall.jpg/220px-Seaton_Beach_after_heavy_rainfall.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Seaton_Beach_after_heavy_rainfall.jpg/330px-Seaton_Beach_after_heavy_rainfall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Seaton_Beach_after_heavy_rainfall.jpg/440px-Seaton_Beach_after_heavy_rainfall.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Seaton_Beach_after_heavy_rainfall.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The mouth of the <a href="/wiki/River_Seaton" title="River Seaton">River Seaton</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a> after heavy rainfall caused flooding in the area and cause a significant amount of the beach to erode; leaving behind a tall sand bank in its place</div></div></div>
<p>At extremely high flows, <a href="/wiki/Kolk_(vortex)" title="Kolk (vortex)">kolks</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Vortex" title="Vortex">vortices</a> are formed by large volumes of rapidly rushing water. Kolks cause extreme local erosion, plucking bedrock and creating pothole-type geographical features called <a href="/wiki/Rock-cut_basin" title="Rock-cut basin">rock-cut basins</a>. Examples can be seen in the flood regions result from glacial <a href="/wiki/Lake_Missoula" title="Lake Missoula">Lake Missoula</a>, which created the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Channeled_scablands" title="Channeled scablands">channeled scablands</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Columbia_River_Drainage_Basin" title="Columbia River Drainage Basin">Columbia Basin</a> region of eastern <a href="/wiki/Washington_(state)" title="Washington (state)">Washington</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wind_erosion">Wind erosion</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Im_Salar_de_Uyuni.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1544" data-file-width="2080" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Im_Salar_de_Uyuni.jpg/220px-Im_Salar_de_Uyuni.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Im_Salar_de_Uyuni.jpg/330px-Im_Salar_de_Uyuni.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Im_Salar_de_Uyuni.jpg/440px-Im_Salar_de_Uyuni.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Im_Salar_de_Uyuni.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/%C3%81rbol_de_Piedra" title="Árbol de Piedra">Árbol de Piedra</a>, a rock formation in the <a href="/wiki/Altiplano" title="Altiplano">Altiplano</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a> sculpted by wind erosion</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Aeolian_processes" title="Aeolian processes">Aeolian processes</a></div>
<p>Wind erosion is a major <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geomorphological" title="Geomorphological">geomorphological</a> force, especially in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arid_region" title="Arid region">arid</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semi-arid_region" title="Semi-arid region">semi-arid</a> regions. It is also a major source of land degradation, evaporation, desertification, harmful airborne dust, and crop damage—especially after being increased far above natural rates by human activities such as <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Urbanization" title="Urbanization">urbanization</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>Wind erosion is of two primary varieties: <i><a href="/wiki/Aeolian_processes#Wind_erosion" title="Aeolian processes">deflation</a></i>, where the wind picks up and carries away loose particles; and <i><a href="/wiki/Abrasion_(geology)" title="Abrasion (geology)">abrasion</a></i>, where <a href="/wiki/Erosion_surface" title="Erosion surface">surfaces</a> are worn down as they are struck by airborne particles carried by wind. Deflation is divided into three categories: (1) <i><a href="/wiki/Downhill_creep" title="Downhill creep">surface creep</a></i>, where larger, heavier particles slide or roll along the ground; (2) <i><a href="/wiki/Saltation_(geology)" title="Saltation (geology)">saltation</a></i>, where particles are lifted a short height into the air, and bounce and saltate across the surface of the soil; and (3) <i><a href="/wiki/Suspension_(chemistry)" title="Suspension (chemistry)">suspension</a></i>, where very small and light particles are lifted into the air by the wind, and are often carried for long distances. Saltation is responsible for the majority (50-70%) of wind erosion, followed by suspension (30-40%), and then surface creep (5-25%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BlancoWind_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-BlancoWind-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 57">: 57 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>Wind erosion is much more severe in arid areas and during times of drought. For example, in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Plains" title="Great Plains">Great Plains</a>, it is estimated that soil loss due to wind erosion can be as much as 6100 times greater in drought years than in wet years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mass_wasting">Mass wasting</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NegevWadi2009.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/NegevWadi2009.JPG/220px-NegevWadi2009.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/NegevWadi2009.JPG/330px-NegevWadi2009.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/NegevWadi2009.JPG/440px-NegevWadi2009.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NegevWadi2009.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Wadi" title="Wadi">wadi</a> in <a href="/wiki/Makhtesh_Ramon" title="Makhtesh Ramon">Makhtesh Ramon</a>, Israel, showing gravity collapse erosion on its banks</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mass_wasting" title="Mass wasting">Mass wasting</a></div>
<p><i>Mass wasting</i> or <i>mass movement</i> is the downward and outward movement of rock and sediments on a sloped surface, mainly due to the force of <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mass wasting is an important part of the erosional process and is often the first stage in the breakdown and transport of weathered materials in mountainous areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nichols_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nichols-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 93">: 93 </span></sup> It moves material from higher elevations to lower elevations where other eroding agents such as streams and <a href="/wiki/Glacier" title="Glacier">glaciers</a> can then pick up the material and move it to even lower elevations. Mass-wasting processes are always occurring continuously on all slopes; some mass-wasting processes act very slowly; others occur very suddenly, often with disastrous results. Any perceptible down-slope movement of rock or sediment is often referred to in general terms as a <a href="/wiki/Landslide" title="Landslide">landslide</a>. However, landslides can be classified in a much more detailed way that reflects the mechanisms responsible for the movement and the velocity at which the movement occurs. One of the visible topographical manifestations of a very slow form of such activity is a <a href="/wiki/Scree" title="Scree">scree</a> slope.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Slump_(geology)" title="Slump (geology)">Slumping</a></i> happens on steep hillsides, occurring along distinct fracture zones, often within materials like <a href="/wiki/Clay" title="Clay">clay</a> that, once released, may move quite rapidly downhill. They will often show a spoon-shaped <a href="/wiki/Isostatic_depression" title="Isostatic depression">isostatic depression</a>, in which the material has begun to slide downhill. In some cases, the slump is caused by water beneath the slope weakening it. In many cases it is simply the result of poor engineering along <a href="/wiki/Highway" title="Highway">highways</a> where it is a regular occurrence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P.2007_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-P.2007-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Surface creep</i> is the slow movement of soil and rock debris by gravity which is usually not perceptible except through extended observation. However, the term can also describe the rolling of dislodged soil particles 0.5 to 1.0 mm (0.02 to 0.04 in) in diameter by wind along the soil surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Submarine_sediment_gravity_flows">Submarine sediment gravity flows</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CanyonsbathyLG_USGS.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1255" data-file-width="1040" decoding="async" height="265" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/CanyonsbathyLG_USGS.jpg/220px-CanyonsbathyLG_USGS.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/CanyonsbathyLG_USGS.jpg/330px-CanyonsbathyLG_USGS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/CanyonsbathyLG_USGS.jpg/440px-CanyonsbathyLG_USGS.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CanyonsbathyLG_USGS.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bathymetry" title="Bathymetry">Bathymetry</a> of submarine canyons in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Continental_slope" title="Continental slope">continental slope</a> off the coast of New York and New Jersey</div></div></div>
<p>On the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Continental_slope" title="Continental slope">continental slope</a>, erosion of the ocean floor to create channels and <a href="/wiki/Submarine_canyon" title="Submarine canyon"> submarine canyons</a> can result from the rapid downslope flow of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sediment_gravity_flows" title="Sediment gravity flows"> sediment gravity flows</a>, bodies of sediment-laden water that move rapidly downslope as <a href="/wiki/Turbidity_current" title="Turbidity current"> turbidity currents</a>.   Where erosion by turbidity currents creates oversteepened slopes it can also trigger underwater landslides and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Debris_flows" title="Debris flows"> debris flows</a>.   Turbidity currents can erode channels and canyons into substrates ranging from recently deposited unconsolidated sediments to hard crystalline bedrock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>   Almost all continental slopes and deep ocean basins display such channels and canyons resulting from sediment gravity flows and submarine canyons act as conduits for the transfer of sediment from the continents and shallow marine environments to the deep sea. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turbidites" title="Turbidites">Turbidites</a>, which are the sedimentary deposits resulting from turbidity currents, comprise some of the thickest and largest sedimentary sequences on Earth, indicating that the associated erosional processes must also have played a prominent role in Earth's history.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Factors_affecting_erosion_rates">Factors affecting erosion rates</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1045330069">.mw-parser-output .sidebar{width:22em;float:right;clear:right;margin:0.5em 0 1em 1em;background:#f8f9fa;border:1px solid <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Climatic_geomorphology" title="Climatic geomorphology">Climatic geomorphology</a></div>
<p>The amount and intensity of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rainfall" title="Rainfall">precipitation</a> is the main <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climatic factor</a> governing soil erosion by water. The relationship is particularly strong if heavy rainfall occurs at times when, or in locations where, the soil's surface is not well protected by <a href="/wiki/Vegetation" title="Vegetation">vegetation</a>. This might be during periods when <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agricultural activities</a> leave the soil bare, or in <a href="/wiki/Semi-arid_climate" title="Semi-arid climate">semi-arid</a> regions where vegetation is naturally sparse. Wind erosion requires strong winds, particularly during times of drought when vegetation is sparse and soil is dry (and so is more erodible). Other climatic factors such as average temperature and temperature range may also affect erosion, via their effects on vegetation and soil properties. In general, given similar vegetation and ecosystems, areas with more precipitation (especially high-intensity rainfall), more wind, or more storms are expected to have more erosion.
</p><p>In some areas of the world (e.g. the <a href="/wiki/Midwestern_United_States" title="Midwestern United States">mid-western USA</a>), rainfall intensity is the primary determinant of erosivity (for a definition of <i>erosivity</i> check,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>) with higher intensity rainfall generally resulting in more soil erosion by water. The size and velocity of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rain_drop" title="Rain drop">rain drops</a> is also an important factor. Larger and higher-velocity rain drops have greater <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a>, and thus their impact will displace soil particles by larger distances than smaller, slower-moving rain drops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BlancoWater_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-BlancoWater-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>In other regions of the world (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">western Europe</a>), runoff and erosion result from relatively low intensities of <a href="/wiki/Precipitation_types#Stratiform" title="Precipitation types">stratiform rainfall</a> falling onto the previously saturated soil. In such situations, rainfall amount rather than intensity is the main factor determining the severity of soil erosion by water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boardman_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Boardman-17">[17]</a></sup> According to the climate change projections, erosivity will increase significantly in Europe and soil erosion may increase by 13-22.5% by 2050 <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>, where typhoon frequency increased significantly in the 21st century, a strong link has been drawn between the increase in storm frequency with an increase in sediment load in rivers and reservoirs, highlighting the impacts <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> can have on erosion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vegetative_cover">Vegetative cover</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Vegetation_and_slope_stability" title="Vegetation and slope stability">Vegetation and slope stability</a></div>
<p>Vegetation acts as an interface between the atmosphere and the soil. It increases the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Permeability_(earth_sciences)" title="Permeability (earth sciences)">permeability</a> of the soil to rainwater, thus decreasing runoff. It shelters the soil from winds, which results in decreased wind erosion, as well as advantageous changes in microclimate. The roots of the plants bind the soil together, and interweave with other roots, forming a more solid mass that is less susceptible to both water<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> and wind erosion. The removal of vegetation increases the rate of surface erosion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Topography">Topography</span></h3>
<p>The topography of the land determines the velocity at which <a href="/wiki/Surface_runoff" title="Surface runoff">surface runoff</a> will flow, which in turn determines the erosivity of the runoff. Longer, steeper slopes (especially those without adequate vegetative cover) are more susceptible to very high rates of erosion during heavy rains than shorter, less steep slopes. Steeper terrain is also more prone to mudslides, landslides, and other forms of gravitational erosion processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BlancoWater_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-BlancoWater-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 28–30">: 28–30 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tectonics">Tectonics</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Erosion_and_tectonics" title="Erosion and tectonics">Erosion and tectonics</a></div>
<p>Tectonic processes control rates and distributions of erosion at the Earth's surface. If the tectonic action causes part of the Earth's surface (e.g., a mountain range) to be raised or lowered relative to surrounding areas, this must necessarily change the gradient of the land surface. Because erosion rates are almost always sensitive to the local slope (see above), this will change the rates of erosion in the uplifted area. Active tectonics also brings fresh, unweathered rock towards the surface, where it is exposed to the action of erosion.
</p><p>However, erosion can also affect tectonic processes. The removal by erosion of large amounts of rock from a particular region, and its deposition elsewhere, can result in a lightening of the load on the <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">lower crust</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a>. Because tectonic processes are driven by gradients in the stress field developed in the crust, this unloading can in turn cause <a href="/wiki/Tectonic_uplift" title="Tectonic uplift">tectonic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Isostasy" title="Isostasy">isostatic uplift</a> in the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nichols_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nichols-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 99">: 99 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> In some cases, it has been hypothesised that these twin feedbacks can act to localize and enhance zones of very rapid exhumation of deep crustal rocks beneath places on the Earth's surface with extremely high erosion rates, for example, beneath the extremely steep terrain of <a href="/wiki/Nanga_Parbat" title="Nanga Parbat">Nanga Parbat</a> in the western <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Himalaya" title="Himalaya">Himalayas</a>. Such a place has been called a "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/River_anticlines#Tectonic_aneurysms" title="River anticlines">tectonic aneurysm</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span></h3>
<p>Human land development, in forms including agricultural and urban development, is considered a significant factor in erosion and <a href="/wiki/Sediment_transport" title="Sediment transport">sediment transport</a>, which aggravate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_insecurity" title="Food insecurity">food insecurity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> In Taiwan, increases in sediment load in the northern, central, and southern regions of the island can be tracked with the timeline of development for each region throughout the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-63">[63]</a></sup> The intentional removal of soil and rock by humans is a form of erosion that has been named <i><a href="/wiki/Lisasion" title="Lisasion">lisasion</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Erosion_at_various_scales">Erosion at various scales</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mountain_ranges">Mountain ranges</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Denudation" title="Denudation">denudation</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planation" title="Planation">planation</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Mountain_range" title="Mountain range">Mountain ranges</a> are known to take many millions of years to erode to the degree they effectively cease to exist. Scholars Pitman and Golovchenko estimate that it takes probably more than 450 million years to erode a mountain mass similar to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Himalaya" title="Himalaya">Himalaya</a> into an almost-flat <a href="/wiki/Peneplain" title="Peneplain">peneplain</a> if there are no major <a href="/wiki/Sea_level#Change" title="Sea level">sea-level changes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PitmanGolovchenko1991_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-PitmanGolovchenko1991-72">[72]</a></sup> Erosion of mountains massifs can create a pattern of equally high summits called <a href="/wiki/Summit_accordance" title="Summit accordance">summit accordance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> It has been argued that <a href="/wiki/Extensional_tectonics" title="Extensional tectonics">extension</a> during <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Post-orogenic_collapse" title="Post-orogenic collapse">post-orogenic collapse</a> is a more effective mechanism of lowering the height of orogenic mountains than erosion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deweyetal1993_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Deweyetal1993-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>Examples of heavily eroded mountain ranges include the <a href="/wiki/Timanide_Orogen" title="Timanide Orogen">Timanides</a> of Northern Russia. Erosion of this <a href="/wiki/Orogeny" title="Orogeny">orogen</a> has produced <a href="/wiki/Sediment" title="Sediment">sediments</a> that are now found in the <a href="/wiki/East_European_Platform" title="East European Platform">East European Platform</a>, including the Cambrian <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Sablya_Formation&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Sablya Formation (page does not exist)">Sablya Formation</a> near <a href="/wiki/Lake_Ladoga" title="Lake Ladoga">Lake Ladoga</a>. Studies of these sediments indicate that it is likely that the erosion of the orogen began in the Cambrian and then intensified in the <a href="/wiki/Ordovician" title="Ordovician">Ordovician</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Orlovetal2011_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Orlovetal2011-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Soils">Soils</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Soil_erosion" title="Soil erosion">soil erosion</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pedogenesis" title="Pedogenesis">pedogenesis</a></div>
<p>If the rate of erosion is higher than the rate of soil formation the soils are being destroyed by erosion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Migon_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Migon-76">[76]</a></sup> Where soil is not destroyed by erosion, erosion can in some cases prevent the formation of soil features that form slowly. <a href="/wiki/Inceptisol" title="Inceptisol">Inceptisols</a> are common soils that form in areas of fast erosion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>While erosion of soils is a natural process, human activities have increased by 10-40 times the rate at which erosion is occurring globally. Excessive (or accelerated) erosion causes both "on-site" and "off-site" problems. On-site impacts include decreases in <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_productivity" title="Agricultural productivity">agricultural productivity</a> and (on <a href="/wiki/Natural_landscape" title="Natural landscape">natural landscapes</a>) <a href="/wiki/Ecological_collapse" title="Ecological collapse">ecological collapse</a>, both because of loss of the nutrient-rich upper <a href="/wiki/Soil_horizon" title="Soil horizon">soil layers</a>. In some cases, the eventual end result is <a href="/wiki/Desertification" title="Desertification">desertification</a>. Off-site effects include <a href="/wiki/Sediment#Erosion_and_agricultural_sediment_delivery_to_rivers" title="Sediment">sedimentation of waterways</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eutrophication" title="Eutrophication">eutrophication</a> of water bodies, as well as sediment-related damage to roads and houses. Water and wind erosion are the two primary causes of <a href="/wiki/Land_degradation" title="Land degradation">land degradation</a>; combined, they are responsible for about 84% of the global extent of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Degraded_land" title="Degraded land">degraded land</a>, making excessive erosion one of the most significant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_problems" title="Environmental problems">environmental problems</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toy-2002-p1_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-toy-2002-p1-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Springer_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Springer-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United States, farmers cultivating <a href="/wiki/Highly_erodible_land" title="Highly erodible land">highly erodible land</a> must comply with a conservation plan to be eligible for certain forms of agricultural assistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Consequences_of_human-made_soil_erosion">Consequences of human-made soil erosion</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">Human impact on the environment</a>, <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_agriculture" title="Environmental impact of agriculture">Environmental impact of agriculture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soil_retrogression_and_degradation" title="Soil retrogression and degradation">Soil retrogression and degradation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Land_degradation" title="Land degradation">Land degradation</a></div>
